0375826742|3.0|As another tome necessary in the process of telling the author's story, it fulfills his expectations, I think, quite adequately. The continuation of a grand war of many against an overwhelming evil is carried out quite well. As the arrogant and despicable Galbatorix wants more power over all aspects of people's lives, the comparison with today's world of governments that want the same, is impossible to ignore. What this volume provides is hope in the heart of those who wish to preserve their individual freedoms and thwart this devilish-seeming invulvnerable grasp for power and to fight it effectively requires persistence, guile and the transparency of truth to win out against the heavy hand of overgovernance.<br /><br />The action scenes are good, but some of the description of events like coronations and long-winded discussions of strategies seem, at times, like the author has become very unfocused and susceptible to currents of vague mistiness resulting in a lack of clarity.  Three areas that more specific development would help (not through the use of many, many, many words, but using brief words of clarity) are those of more romance, effective characterization, and a diminution of Eragon's unending self-doubt.<br /><br />Really, unless we are talking about prepubescent teens as the main readers, romance and physical attraction should have been inserted into the main themes of this weighty novel, making it more grounded in people's reality and desires, and our ability to grasp the story. There are characters calling out for these encounters, such as between Eragon and Arya, Angela and...someone, etc. that would flesh out this lack of content that would help us believe more strongly in the 'reality' of this fantasy.  The lack of its insertion makes me think there is a bit of arrested development in the whole conception and implementation of the story, that keeps it from taking on substance and leaves the misty mindedness that is good for only the early developmentof a story. Let there be romance in all of our life, else we are bereft of a human (or elf) quality that we can identify with and understand, and give us a more full-fleshed reality to bring this story into our hearts.<br /><br />The second area needing help is the characters. Yes, there is a lot of character development, too much that is lightly done and superficial, so that it is easy to lose sight of who's who in such a long story, and results in a lack of true caring by the reader for them. Again, this is an example of the stupefying quantity of characters provided, instead of a true focus on a few characters developed fully, to give us reason to care about their well-being. The book did not do a quality, thorough, character development early, so we can see the main individuals clearly, touch them and breath in their body aromas in words that clearly leave us excited about them (whether for good or bad).  Later the author should've continued developing them in small ways throughout the story, as needed to full develop them, but using quality development in brief reactions to events, strengths or weakenesses that are charming and endearing, not those that weaken the character. Re-characterizing poorly developed individuals in a later chapter (going over the same traits in a lackluster way) is not giving your readers the credit they've already understand those traits and carry them forward with them. Shouldn't have rehashed the lightly done traits again and again.<br /><br />The last is the seeming need for long-winded explanations of the protagonist's self-doubt. If you have someone who is human you always have self-doubt. But at times the author brings this internal questioning to a point that it almost seems like torture.  Who in the right mind would want to follow such a self-effacing, effeminate leader who purports to be a major leader? It is the personal strength of character and leadership that people follow. Instead the author tends to use artifice such as magic to account for almost everything that is harder to develp and explain. Perhaps the reason for the weakness of Eragon is based in the unfortunate weaving of one of today's destructive presences, PC thought, that makes it difficult to really admire someone like Eragon. There seems to be such a leavening of PC thought that contaminates this whole work, and at times, makes me want to scream, 'Enough!' and throw the book down for days at a time, before picking it back up and, hopefully, believe that it isn't really as prevalent and destructive to a book I so  much want to admire. Less PC and more true humanity.<br /><br />I gave it three out of five, but, if I were in a period of mood blackness due to the aversion to PC thought, I could have easily gone to a two star level. Clearly, that would've been unfair to the parts of the book that are very well done. Compromise is ever a part of what we do in today's world, unfortunately, instead of reaching for true perfection.

B006O5YHUY|5.0|Outstanding movie! This was a great surprise.<br /><br />As a \\"fighting movie\\", it was refreshing to see true depth to a story outside of the ring in parallel to the one taking place inside. The acting was phenomenal by Edgerton, Hardy, and Nolte, though Hardy stole it for me (this guy is amazing). The music was right on cue and fit the scenes. The fight scenes were good enough get out of my seat and cheer. The drama was masculine, a bit dark, and cauldron deep.<br /><br />I love that there was NOTHING gratuitous or cheap in this movie. In a day where movie makers try too hard to appeal to all demographics and end up cheapening what could have been a great movie (Transformers), this sets a new bar for balance. The fight candy only enhances the drama, and vice versa. I saw little in the way of compromise in either. With a strong story and good actors, you really don't need CGI, sex, F-bombs every other word, etc.<br /><br />To beautifully wrap up a great movie, the two stories fully converge in THE MOMENT. It is in the final fight and is the true climax of the movie. As a man, that moment is unmistakable, unforgettable, and unbelievably powerful, because it was impossible not to be strongly sympathetic to the characters by that time. It was expertly built up and served to make the fight become progressively gut wrenching and sad as we recognize what winning will actually cost the other. Though, unfortunately, losing still did not seem to be an option, given the noble reasons both were supposed to be fighting for in the first place.<br /><br />I also saw the last fight as a culminating metaphor for life. The message relating to how much of our pain and anguish is self inflicted, though many of us still double-down in treading our path alone and on our own terms, notwithstanding our misery. Eventually there comes a point where we may recognize that the true cost of \\"doubling-down\\" is actually greater than \\"tapping-out\\" (conceding, forgiving, repenting). In such a moment of great fear and vulnerability, we need someone to comfort us, someone to make it ok to let go. Only then can we see that true \\"victory\\" (peace, joy - whatever that may mean to us) was never to be found alone. For this reason, the fight was absolutely necessary to complete the story. This is also why I think most viewers, including myself, at least got \\"a bit choked up\\" in this scene.<br /><br />It is not every day that you see a profoundly counterintuitive spiritual principle masterfully captured by a \\"fight movie\\", or any movie for that matter.<br /><br />I hadn't even heard of this movie until yesterday. Lucky me!<br />Lucky you, if you watch it.

0553386794|3.0|Writing Quality: 6/10.<br />Martin is a fine writer. Not great, but his prose doesn't have most of the really egregious mistakes that a lot of other bestselling fantasy writers have, whether we're talking about Robert Jordan, Terry Brooks, or worst of all, Terry Goodkind. At the same time, I don't think a single phrase in the book struck me as anything worth remembering, and that's something I usually look for. Tolkien, by comparison, doesn't have much more ability as a wordsmith, except that every once in a while he used a turn of phrase that stuck tenaciously in his readers' heads, after years or even decades (\\"All who wander are not lost,\\" for instance). I almost scored Martin a 5 (which would put him a point lower than Tolkien in The Fellowship of the Ring), but then I decided he employed a really exceptional ability to weave together varied character story lines so that they all felt connected and driving towards a common end (which Tolkien struggled with, I think).<br /><br />Depth of Concept: 6/10.<br />I debated whether this was a 6 or a 7, and ultimately chose a 6 even though Martin clearly is exploring original concepts and making a legitimate bid for breaking out of the typical fantasy formula, which so few of his bestselling peers bother to do. This is a detailed world with complex themes involved, feeling as though it's lifted straight from the power struggles of the War of the Roses. In any given character you'll find competing impulses of ambition, loyalty, love, selfishness, nobility, and cowardice. Nevertheless, it's still not a very subtle work. A 6 means that Martin is intelligent and his story presents issues that are worthy of stopping for a minute to think about.<br /><br />Rounded Characters: 6/10.<br />Martin's character development is a cut above most writing in the fantasy genre, especially those hitting the bestseller lists. Most of the comments in the above category could apply here as well. Still, you don't find characters really learning or changing in particularly insightful ways, only adapting logistically as situations arise. You don't have a Gollum character to carry the nuanced weight of tragedy and redemption, or an Ishmael/Ahab binary to explore the metaphysically ambiguous limits of devil and savior, or a character like the Man in Cormac McCarthy's The Road who experiences with heartbreaking tenderness, pain, sacrifice, and love in the context of a hellish world. Martin's characters are respectably rounded, but rarely exceptional. There are also A LOT of characters, though it didn't take me too long to settle into the detailed world of warring families and dynasties.<br /><br />Well-Developed World: 7/10.<br />Although Martin's Song of Ice and Fire series unabashedly uses the British Isles and surrounding lands as its model, it does so in just the right amount to help us situate our understanding of the world, and then adds in many delightful analogs and divergences. It's almost an alternate history, but without relying excessively on the real-world source as a crutch. It's an appreciable re-imagining that allows us to incorporate our historical understanding of the land with more fantastic elements, and it's a really engaging setting that uses the geographical and climate differences of different regions to justifiably forward the plot. This is a world that hints at the reality of magic and otherworldly elements, but that lives or dies by its political intrigue and power shifts, some through back-room machinations and some through direct confrontations.<br /><br />Page Turner: 9/10.<br />This is unarguably where Martin's works stand out the most. I can't think of any other author I've read who switches points of view so frequently (Martin does it every chapter), and can keep my interest so consistently through the conclusion of one point of view and into the next. There's a really smooth flow from one setting to the next, such that you rarely feel like you're reading about unrelated events or stories. Martin manages to infuse back-room dealings with at least the intensity and pacing that you find in the battle scenes. And while his characters may not do a lot of growing (with the exception of some nice scenes between adolescent boys training for military service on the \\"Wall,\\" and an exiled princess who comes into her own as a queen) during the novel, their actions are frequently as unpredictable as any real-life character, happily without becoming unbelievable. You'll frequently find yourself uncertain whether a character will be alive or dead by the end of the chapter, and Martin does in fact kill off charismatic characters from time to time. There are those who are turned off by protagonists who are not always pure and good; but if you are engaged by morally grey scenarios in which people of all types struggle to abide by their own value systems, this novel will keep you on the edge of your seat. Here are a few comparison points: David Eddings writes very formulaic characters, both good and evil (although I admit that I still enjoy his novels, and I loved them as a kid). Robert Jordan hints at more Machiavellian characters, but there are still very clear lines drawn between characters on the \\"good\\" spectrum and those on the \\"bad\\" spectrum. Terry Goodkind is perhaps the greatest abuser of the perfectly good protagonist, the most brave, sacrificing, honest, and \\"pure\\" character. If you're ready for more realistic characters who struggle to do the right thing (and often don't), who believe in codes and creeds but don't always follow them, who make decisions that inadvertently harm themselves or their families, then Martin's story will do something for you that these other authors simply don't aspire to, or aren't capable of presenting.<br /><br />Kept Me Thinking: 5/10.<br />Some might argue that a real page turner is inevitably balanced by being less of a thinking book, or vice versa, but I think that's a cop-out. When I read anything by Cormac McCarthy, I find it almost impossible to tear myself away, and yet The Road (or All the Pretty Horses, or anything else he's written) is just brimming with material that'll keep your heart and intellect preoccupied long after putting the thing down. Martin's isn't terrible for a thinking person . . . but I think of it as engaging entertainment that you won't feel guilty about even if it's not being used as airport reading material.<br />___________________________________________________<br /><br />Overall Recommendation: 6/10.<br />This is a tricky one to recommend for the following reason: Martin uses sex (and violence, but violence bothers far fewer people) in his novels just the way you'd expect a studio to use sex in rated-R movies.I've read other reviews where people claim that he's merely describing a gritty world, but Martin's descriptions go beyond realism and into a kind of sleazy territory. It's not just that there is an actual or remembered rape in every chapter, including gang rapes and incest and rape involving children. Raping and pillaging is a historical fact, and I respect that those things deserve some acknowledgement. McCarthy's The Road, Toni Morrison's Beloved, and even Nabokov's Lolita treat difficult subjects involving violence and sexuality with thoughtful and complicated narratives. But Martin spends an inordinate amount of time describing genitalia, bodily fluids, and sex sounds, all to a kind of creepy extent. Perhaps even more troubling is the pervasive theme of rape and prostitution as a status quo in all walks of life, discussed by most characters with something approaching glee. I can respect that the status of women in the time period Martin seeks to describe was legitimately troubled; and yet Martin's descriptions seem to objectify women with little effort to complicate the debasement he describes. Now, if you're the kind of person who prefers HBO shows because of the extra skin and blood they flaunt, or if you aren't particularly sensitive to rape everywhere you look, you'll have no problems here. If you're a little less comfortable with the woman as sex object, you might want to think twice about this series, despite its other commendable qualities. At the least, these concerns are something every reader should think about, and to the extent that you indict or exonerate Martin on this topic, the overall recommendation for this book should drop or rise at least a point from what I've listed here.<br /><br />See my profile web page for an expanded review (as though this weren't already long enough!)arthy, I find it almost impossible to tear myself away, and yet The Road (or All the Pretty Horses, or anything else he's written) is just brimming with material that'll keep your heart and intellect preoccupied long after putting the thing down. Quick fame) following up on the critically acclaimed comeback album Blood Of Nations from 2010.<br /><br />The quality of the songwriting is excellent and the band make a superb job of mixing ballsy up-tempo tracks of purely driving classic Heavy Metal and longer dynamic tracks that shift tempos and explore clean and acoustic territory as well.  There is enough variety so it isn't all just the same, but enough consistency that you're getting a pure and succinct experience.<br /><br />Like Blood Of Nations before it, Stalingrad takes the classic Accept sound and modernizes it slightly. Don't misunderstand, it isn't overly contrived to be youthful, but it is energetic and vital with any cheesiness removed. The record touches on a few metal styles, with hints of Thrash, Speed and Power Metal all creeping in in small doses alongside the classic Teutonic Metal style off the early Accept albums.<br /><br />Basically; imagine all the best elements of bands like Judas Priest, Iron Maiden, Motorhead, Megadeth, Overkill, and others condensed down into one album of tasteful guitar melodies and soaring leads, gang-backing-vocals and hard crunchy riffs. Mark's gravely low voice and occasional high notes come across as a brilliant mid-way point between Lemmy influenced singers and Halford influenced singers and suit the music remarkably well.<br /><br />The production, from Andy Sneap is also utterly perfect for the album and makes the music sound both powerful and clear, with crunchy feeling on the palm muted riffs and a clean sound on the leads. It isn't as dynamic as a 70s or 80s production job but it is a fantastic example of what a modern job can sound like when done right.<br /><br />Overall, if you like Accept and especially their new line-up then this is a must-have record; if you like traditional heavy metal then this is really worth exploring and finally if you like any other metal in general then you should consider giving this record a chance. Its solid, its meaty, it's the perfect mixture of very Hard Rock and straight-up Metal and I highly recommend it.y metal then this is really worth exploring and finally if you like any other metal in general then you should consider giving this record a chance. B0056K4VRM|2.0|Another gentleman reviewing this set has called it a steal. The price, though undeniably enticing, is not the only thing about the set that screams CHEAP. The real steal, in some ways the only steal, is that of the consumer's cash, taken in return for third-rate transfers of well-produced stereo recordings from 1960 and thereabouts.<br /><br />Of the symphonies, the best-sounding of the present lot are the two recordings that always sounded the best: the Haffner and the Jupiter. Still, absence of surface noise aside, neither sounds better than the LP originals, which were singled out as remarkably good products at their initial release. What is more, neither these two symphonies nor 38, 39, and 40 sound as good as earlier Sony CD releases from the mid-eighties and early nineties. After having done numerous A-B comparisons of them all, I can report that in this new edition the latter three works sound like pressings made from a copy of the production master for the older [[ASIN:B0000027A8 Legendary Interpretations set]]. Tonal balance and relative instrumental prominence are the same, but all the newer discs have a slight to noticeable veil over the music, a veil not present earlier. It's a safe bet that the new pressings were not made from the original masters or first-generation copies.<br /><br />Worst for last: the remastering of the Linz Symphony is simply horrible. Imagine that someone made a cassette dubbing, on supercheap Type 1 tape, of his dad's scratchy old LP, already played a thousand times. Then he handed the cassette over to the chief of marketing at SonyUSA. He in turn gave it to the producer of this set and said, \\"Hey, Irv, use this thing to make the production master; the idiot buyers will never notice.\\"<br /><br />Of the other releases of this recording that I own--four in all: two LPs and two CDs--the two best are the remastered Japanese Sony LP from the late seventies and the very first domestic CBS CD (MK42027), which was produced from the Japanese LP master. Both are vastly superior to the Linz in this set, which I shall never listen to again. Anyone who thinks (1) that this stereo Linz recording is the best interpretation among Walter's three available recordings (counting the ubiquitous 1956 Paris aircheck), (2) that this is the Mozart symphony he did best of all, and (3) that no one has ever done the Linz as well as Walter--my own hand is up in all three cases--will be reduced to tears of frustration by the present issue.<br /><br />Incidentally, I have heard but never owned two releases of the stereo Linz that are measurably superior to all others. The first is the original open-reel tape corresponding to the stereo LP release of fifty years ago (DDD idolaters will bitterly complain about the very low but steady background tape hiss, of course). The second is [[ASIN:B000MAFYHC the current Japanese high-tech remastering]] (not SACD, alas), but just thinking about it leaves me feeling too strapped to buy lunch.<br /><br />It is sad that Sony's American and European branches now treat this conductor's legacy with such open contempt. When one compares the solicitude with which RCA, DG, and EMI have treated their archives of Toscanini (for the first-named company) and Furtw&auml;ngler recordings (for the latter two), Sony's attitude becomes truly scandalous.<br /><br />This conclusion is, if anything, exacerbated by the fact that the Japanese Sony remasterings and re-releases of the Walter material over the decades have been stupendously good, true state-of-the-art products. The problem for anyone on this side of the Pacific, of course, is that he needs to phone his financial adviser before daring to invest in any of them.<br /><br />The other recordings duplicated herein are happily not so badly handled, yet even so my A-B comparisons show that they utilize copies of earlier masters (some from the long-departed Bruno Walter Edition) rather than those masters themselves. Nothing stands as the equal of an earlier release, let alone an improvement on it. The Requiem is a case in point. The [[ASIN:B000002A86 BW edition]] had succeeded in finally, after forty years, letting some light shine through the aural murk and fog of Carnegie Hall, this recording's venue; the present CD, unfortunately, restores about half of the tenebrous gloom. Still, the interpretation is profound, the orchestra brilliant, and the singing of Irmgard Seefried and L&eacute;opold Simoneau seldom matched. The violin concerto recordings, with Zino Francescatti the perfect partner, are also not a disappointment; indeed, they are the least impaired of all the tapings reproduced in this set.<br /><br />Those unfamiliar with Bruno Walter's conducting may be wondering, \\"Why the fuss?\\" The answer: because aside from the G minor (K. 550), all the symphonies receive great performances, in a style that is inimitably individual yet formed in a tradition now, alas, dead as the dodo. Even the G minor is well worth an audition, but the way Walter hears this music seems oddly ablated from its plain-as-day dramatic implications. All four of his commercial recordings, recorded over a span of thirty-plus years, are similar, whatever their incidental differences of tempo (though even here, despite the impression of greater energy that their more animated tempi bring, none of the earlier performances are as attentive to important details of phrasing and color as the present one). Several airchecks of his frequent live performances are preferable, in that they seem truer to what is on the page. The [[ASIN:B00005B1EA Concertgebouw performance of June 19, 1952]], available on Music & Arts and other labels, is one of a number that show that Walter could call forth this music's dramatic potential without scanting its myriad subtleties.<br /><br />To conclude, listeners who already own CDs containing earlier transfers of the works included here should know that tearing up dollar bills and tossing them to the winds would be more advisable than doing what I did: spend never-to-be-recovered cash on this disappointing reissue. It is certainly recommendable to those who have no other purchasing option, but one does no service to newcomers to Walter's recordings to insist, contrary to all the evidence, that they are getting what they have every right to expect for their money: the best transfers available.<br />_____________<br /><br />Update (March 9, 2015): Late last year, I finally became the owner of a copy of [[ASIN:B000MAFYHC the Japanese high-tech remastering]] of the Linz Symphony (plus no. 39) mentioned above. I was lucky enough to get it used from a Marketplace reseller who, remarkably, was asking less than twenty dollars. It frequently runs to what one might pay for a bottle of vintage claret.<br /><br />All that is worth saying about this disc is that it exhausts superlatives. Since it existed before the present box of CDs was produced, the question that remains is why couldn't Sony have used this issue, along with other current Japanese releases of Walter's Mozart recordings, as the basis for the set under review?is a case in point. B0074EIQQU|4.0|When Omar Rodriguez-Lopez and Cedric Bixler-Zavala began touting their last album, Octahedron, as their &quot;acoustic&quot; album, certain expectations were created in this listener's mind.  When that album was released, those expectations were left almost completely unsatisfied.  Fortunately, my very expectations for Octahedron were realized, almost to a T, in Noctourniquet.  Funny, as the music was apparently recorded around the same time.<br /><br />Having stated the motivation behind Octahedron to be a need for a change in dynamic, what was unveiled instead was more or less usual Mars Volta fare stripped of dynamics.  Stiff, flat compositions performed with seemingly minimal enthusiasm.  It had its moments, but it was altogether disappointing.  Moreover, Bixler-Zavala's work in both the lyrical and melodic departments seemed to be extremely lacking, and at times even dispassionate. I felt more than a little vindicated in these impressions when Mr. Zavala revealed in a recent interview that he is not too fond of the album himself.  In fact, Cedric has opened up about quite a few things in the inner world of The Mars Volta that have served to put a lot of these developments (or lack thereof) in perspective.  For instance, he attributes the aforementioned decline in quality to an oppressive work pace set by his partner-in-crime and erstwhile-musical-dictator, Mr. Lopez.<br /><br />Which brings us to Noctourniquet.  Having &quot;put Omar in his place&quot;, so to speak, Cedric took the better part of 2 years crafting the vocal landscape of the album.  And the payoff is great.  This is by far, in all respects, the band's most consistent and rewarding release since &quot;Frances the Mute&quot;.  The cartoonish excesses of Amputechture and Bedlam have been eliminated, and the lumbering apathy of Octahedron is nowhere to be found.  What we are given is a collection of songs that feel just familiar enough to make longtime fans comfortable while exploring plenty of new territory (Omar writing in major keys?), ranging from the funky electro-pop of &quot;Lapochka&quot;, complete with a wordless, epic shout-along chorus (not to mention some mind bending drum breaks from new drummer Deantoni Parks) to the distorted industrial soundscaping of &quot;In Absentia&quot;, which wouldn't sound out of place alongside &quot;Last Rights&quot;-&quot;Process&quot; era Skinny Puppy.<br /><br />Some of the album's greatest moments, however, are its lighter ones.  &quot;Empty Vessels Make the Loudest Sounds&quot; creeps quietly back and forth like a bipolar ballad between melancholy, brooding passages and bright, major-key, synth-tinged exclamations as Zavala croons, &quot;Must be on my way, I gotta get home..&quot;, perhaps some of the most human words ever spoken in the context of a Mars Volta song, before finally crashing into a heavy and ominous crescendo of &quot;mountains of cavernous people, searching for a lighthouse in the fog.&quot;  This is one of the most beautiful (never thought I'd be using that word to describe a Volta song) pieces of music this band has ever produced, despite also being one of the most minimalistic.<br /><br />The somber, reflective mood established by &quot;Vessels&quot; is quickly dissipated by the metallic squeal that gives birth to the drunken stagger of &quot;The Malkin Jewel&quot;'s psychedelic carnival dub, held in very loose form by the free-rolling drum work of Parks.  Bixler's voice descends to a deep, raspy whisper before rising to a vicious snarl.  This track is odd, even for The Mars Volta, in a very good way.<br /><br />On the other hand, tracks like &quot;Aegis&quot; and &quot;Dyslexicon&quot; provide familiar footing.  After stomping through a militant 4 to the floor march, &quot;Aegis&quot; erupts into a chorus that is strikingly reminiscent of &quot;Inertiatic ESP&quot;.  &quot;Dyslexicon&quot;, meanwhile, spends the first half of its life in an intense post-hardcore grind before winding its way intoa double time jam with that welcome touch of Latin groove.<br /><br />The title track stands alongside &quot;L'Via&quot; and &quot;Meccamputechture&quot; as one of the funkier pieces in Volta's repertoire, and also sports some rather sensual outbursts from Zavala that would make James Brown proud, while &quot;Molochwalker&quot; adds to the ranks of their illustrious aggro-prog catalogue, in the company of &quot;Cygnus.. Vismund Cygnus&quot; and &quot;A Plague Upon Your Hissing&quot;, albeit much tamer than its siblings.  The acoustic explorations of &quot;Trinkets Pale of Moon&quot; and &quot;Vedamalady&quot; offer a brief respite between the two and continue Omar's journey into the softer side of Volta.  Along with &quot;Vedamalady&quot;, &quot;Imago&quot; may be the high water mark of all the acoustic pieces, as they each make ample use of those all-too-alien major key sounds that bring a long overdue shred of light and unabashed prettiness to Volta's typically sinister tonal pallette, focused as it usually is upon tritonal dissonance.<br /><br />Apparently remembering how important first and last impressions can be, the band also does a great job of opening and closing the album on grandiose notes.  Coming out of the gate with &quot;The Whip Hand&quot;, the industrial vibe that will resurface throughout the album is quickly established through the heavy synth work and driving mechanical beat--a simple yet carefully spliced, self-inverting pattern that gives some spice to its 4/4 time.  As the song abruptly transitions into a loud assault of growling, menacing, Reznor-esque synth-lines and crashing cymbals, Bixler wails, on the fringes of tonality, &quot;That's when I disconnect from you.&quot;  This song also boasts what will undoubtedly become THE sing-along line of their live shows, as the band drops out to let Cedric, accompanied only by Parks' infectious beat, proclaim: &quot;I am a landmine, I am a landmine, so don't ya step on me, so don't ya step on me.&quot;<br /><br />Meanwhile, &quot;Zed and Two Naughts&quot; (a nod to experimental filmmaker Peter Greenaway) is, quite simply, the most epic ending of a Volta album since &quot;Take the Veil Cerpin Taxt&quot; (I exclude &quot;Cassandra Gemmini&quot; simply because its 30 minute run time can render it anticlimactic after a while).  The verses sound like a drum'n'bass remix of earlier Volta, while the chorus bears some resemblance to that of &quot;Cassandra Gemmini&quot;.  A perfect blend of the new and the old, and a perfect way to end the album.<br /><br />Overall, Noctourniquet presents the more streamlined Volta that Omar seems to have been envisioning for the past few years without sacrificing any substance.  The slower, acoustic-based ballads are in ample supply, the incessant aural assault has been stifled, new vistas have been explored and claimed, and, most satisfyingly, Cedric presents some of the best vocal melodies of his career, as well as some carefully crafted lyrics that manage to both avoid the recycling and forced verbosity of his recent work, as well as exploring a simpler, more direct side to songwriting.  It also helps to put its awkward predecessor, Octahedron, in perspective.  Instead of working to outdo themselves as they seemed to be for the first several years of their career, these past two releases appear to be somewhat of a weight-loss program for a band growing morbidly obese with musical gluttony.  Where Octahedron was a very unpleasant, painful fast, designed to shed as many pounds of sonic overkill as quickly as possible, Noctourniquet marks a return to a balanced diet that avoids all the excess carbs and sugars.<br /><br />I suppose my one complaint is with the track &quot;Trinkets Pale of Moon.&quot;  Though it's a great song, Cedric's nasally falsetto on the chorus makes me cringe just a little.  But, that's a small complaint for such a great album!  Fans with an eclectic taste should find plenty to sink their teeth into here.have been much more disheartening if they had fizzled out with Octrahedron.  Here's to 12 years of (mostly) great music!  Goodbye, Mars Volta; it was a heck of a ride.
0439023513|1.0|Spoilers here!<br /><br />I wanted this book out of my house the moment I finished reading it, just a stench to Collins handiwork, as if the book itself were one of Snow's roses. I felt cheated by the author. Other reviewers aptly describe how tortured the writing was, how illogical the characters, and unsatisfactory, the storyline.  Thus let me focus specifically on the epilogue:<br /><br />I was so puzzled, annoyed, over the lack of generosity exhibited by Collins, her unwillingness perhaps to re-think her third book, that it became a fixation to re-read the last few chapters to make some sense of the author's choices. I felt on one level I needed to respect the author's imagined final scenes, because, after all, this was her project, brainstorm, pride and joy. Yet somehow, as reviewers often suggested, giving myself permission to interpret the ending she provided, did alleviate the negative emotions, and helped me retain my view of Gale as noble. The epilogue, I concluded, was intentionally bizarre, in that the author had given Katniss a Twilight Zone finish:<br /><br />At first read, it seemed the author had given Katniss a best-case almost-happy ending. Far from it.<br />Katniss is now in more danger than she had ever been, literally at the hands of Peeta. Peeta has to grip a chair at times, holding tight to it, as he tries to squelch the almost overwhelming feeling to strangle<br />Katniss. He can never fully conquer this condition, this impulse.<br /><br />Katniss is a star-crossed lover, who out of guilt denies herself the love of her life, Gale, and who is still<br />so scarred, drug-dazed, that real and unreal are nonsense terms to her. When she replies to Peeta's query if her love for him is real and unreal, she answers \\"real,\\" and she might just as well have said, \\"Whatever.\\" Just pacifying Peeta and rationalizing to herself.<br /><br />The epilogue highlights a feminist message that pervades the entire series that doesn't seem to serve Katniss well anymore. Katniss performs the traditional role of the male, as hunter; Peeta, the classic feminine role, baker/cook. Peeta is not taller than Katniss (and in the film version even shorter) as if he were the old-fashioned \\"little woman\\" of the household. Peeta may be the bread-baker, but Katniss is the bread winner, no rest for her, and there is a weariness in her tone as she speaks of her current family life. Katniss might want to sing to herself something like \\"I am woman,\\" but she has no fight in her to roar. Gone is the possiblity for the life she could have had with Gale, and she knows it, where two hunters worked together, in more of a balanced approach to food-gathering activity (one could rest, enjoy the view, while the other worked at times).<br /><br />Peeta has been patient and waited more than a decade for Katniss to bear children. Yet the result does not<br />signify a warm, loving family. Katniss calls her flesh and blood \\"the girl\\" and \\"the boy,\\" clinically itemizing their physical features, thus displaying a disturbing desensitization to her children. Post-traumatic stress syndrome will never leave her, it seems. Katniss mentions she takes her children to the meadow, which is basically a mass graveyard. She is not clear whether she is speaking of the Hunger Games period, or of an ongoing intra-state conflict, where peace does not seem possible. What is obvious is that the meadow is not a place of innocence or even safety, and she dreads the day she must enlighten these children about its more serious implications.<br /><br />So the ending of the book gives us a wounded non-healer, non-heroine. I agree with other readers who had hoped for a brighter ending. My hope is that someday an enlightened writer of fiction centering on post-apocalyptic themes, will imbibe the spirit of my mentor, St. Francis of Assisi, and show us that a scarred human being could radiate a Franciscan spirit in a frantic world, demonstrating a newfound, undiminished joy Such an ending might not suit everyone's taste, but I like to be uplifted by the works I absorb.

Reading this book was like watching a movie...if you enjoyed The Hunger Games (book #1) and Catching Fire (book #2) as much as I did, you definitely will want to read Mockingjay. I laughed, cried, and felt many other emotions while reading the book. It may only be a story, but Suzanne's words will definitely worm their way in to you...after hearing nothing other than about The Hunger Games for so long, I FINALLY decided to read it and fell in love with it. I highly recommend it...especially if you've already read the other two!! This book is FULL of surprises, and believe me when I say that! You will find yourself pleasantly surprised and itching with anticipation at each turn. This one is a nail-biter...an excellent book. :) The ending of the book, however, did end quite abruptly, which I wasn't thrilled about - i was hoping for a smoother ending than there was. There are some slow parts in the book as well... but all in all, if you read the first two books, you must read this last one. :)
